One pattern deferred: the decay-scored typed-edge memory graph (edges between entries with exponential decay scoring) is noted as a future direction for fctry's memory store — the implementation gap is large and requires local embedding infrastructure not yet available (noted in `#entities` 3.2). One pattern rejected: shadow restore points (pre-write file-level snapshots before each modification). Git-level recovery via per-chunk commits is the existing mechanism; pre-write file snapshots would add complexity without experience benefit.

**Dossier — Per-chunk file scope manifests, uncertainty register, categorized gap analysis, viewer enrichments** (via `/fctry:ref`, 2026-03-07). Source: https://github.com/rwliebs/Dossier. A Claude Code skill for autonomous codebase modernization that packages context per work card (allowed/forbidden file paths, scoped instructions), runs a process check orientation phase before implementation, scores strategy fidelity with named violation categories, and executes via Claude Agent SDK with ephemeral sessions and webhook callbacks. Five patterns adopted into fctry across five spec sections: (1) Per-chunk file scope manifests — each chunk in the build plan includes a predicted file manifest (files to create, modify, touch), visible in the approved plan; the Observer verifies scope compliance post-chunk by comparing the git diff against the manifest, flagging files modified outside the declared scope as scope violations (adapted from Dossier's per-card allowed/forbidden file paths, adopted into `#execute-flow` 2.7 and `#capabilities` 3.1). (2) Per-chunk uncertainty register — before coding each chunk, the Executor documents 3-5 lines categorizing KNOWN (verified facts), ASSUMED (working beliefs), and UNKNOWN (unresolved questions); blocking UNKNOWNs surface as experience questions before coding rather than mid-implementation, shifting failure detection left (adapted from Dossier's process check orientation phase, adopted into `#execute-flow` 2.7). (3) Categorized gap analysis — review findings organized into four named categories (boundary violations, missing implementations, guardrail violations, experience degradations) instead of a flat list, making the kind of drift immediately visible and the review output more actionable (adapted from Dossier's strategy fidelity scoring with named violation categories, adopted into `#review-flow` 2.6). (4) Viewer enrichments — story map visualization showing spec sections as a visual hierarchy with cross-reference connections (complementing kanban's priority view with a relationship view), per-chunk context panel showing the Executor's working knowledge during builds (file manifest, scenarios, uncertainty register), and agent reasoning traces in the activity feed showing the reasoning behind decisions as expandable detail (adapted from Dossier's UI patterns, adopted into `#spec-viewer` 2.9). (5) Scope verification as a named Observer check — the Observer verifies that each chunk only modified files within its declared scope, comparing git diff against the file manifest (adopted into `#capabilities` 3.1). One pattern noted as validating fctry's future direction: Claude Agent SDK direct execution with ephemeral sessions and webhook callbacks validates the background-worker execution model described in `#execute-flow` 2.7, where each chunk could execute as an independent worker with a fresh context window.

**memvid — Version-relation semantics, entity-based indexing** (via `/fctry:ref`, 2026-03-07). Source: https://github.com/memvid/memvid. A Rust-based single-file memory layer for LLMs using semantic chunking, embedding search, and temporal versioning via Memory Cards. Two conceptual patterns adopted into fctry: (1) Version-relation semantics — memvid's Memory Cards carry typed version relations (Sets, Updates, Extends, Retracts) that distinguish between first assignment, full replacement, additive refinement, and negation. fctry adapts this for decision record supersession in the memory store: each new decision declares its relation to the prior entry, making the audit trail more precise than binary supersession. "Extends" (adding a new case) is distinct from "updates" (replacing entirely), and "retracts" (invalidating) is distinct from both. Active recall excludes superseded records from updates and retracts but preserves extended records alongside their extensions (adopted into `#entities` 3.2). (2) Entity-based indexing — memvid indexes recurring concepts as first-class retrieval entities rather than relying solely on structural metadata. fctry notes this as a future retrieval signal for cross-project knowledge: recurring concepts (progressive disclosure, kanban patterns, offline-first architecture) could serve as retrieval entities that bridge projects, complementing the current section-type-based structural similarity matching. When the same concept appears in different structural contexts across projects, entity-based indexing makes it more discoverable than section-alias matching alone (noted as future direction in `#capabilities` 3.1). Implementation rejected: the Rust single-file memory layer introduces a compiled dependency and a different runtime model from fctry's plain markdown memory store with agent-driven operations.

**claude-code-best-practice — Platform architecture validation, native subagent capabilities, community spec-driven convergence** (via `/fctry:ref`, 2026-03-13). Source: https://github.com/shanraisshan/claude-code-best-practice. A community-curated catalog of Claude Code best practices, architecture patterns, and emerging platform capabilities, including a "Billion Dollar Questions" section capturing the community's aspirational use cases. No patterns adopted — all findings confirmatory or noted as platform evolution. Five patterns noted as validating or supplementing existing fctry design: (1) Command-Agent-Skill orchestration architecture (SKILL.md entry point routing to per-command workflows invoking specialized agents) independently validates fctry's progressive disclosure pipeline, confirming the architecture is idiomatic rather than novel. (2) Agent Teams with `isolation: worktree` — native platform mechanism for parallel subagent execution on isolated git worktrees with shared task coordination — noted as the emerging platform path for fctry's background-worker execution future direction (noted in `#execute-flow` 2.7). (3) Native subagent frontmatter capabilities (`model`, `permissionMode`, `maxTurns`, `isolation`, `memory`) — platform features available to the coding agent for agent implementation decisions, supplementing fctry's existing model tier selection and adding turn limits, permission scoping, and agent-level memory as platform primitives (noted in `#agent-decides` 6.4). (4) Native `memory:` frontmatter on agents as a platform supplement to fctry's custom cross-session memory — agent-scoped memory managed by the platform alongside fctry's global memory store (noted in `#multi-session` 2.3). (5) Community convergence on spec-as-source-of-truth — the "Billion Dollar Questions" include "Can we convert a codebase into specs, delete the code, and regenerate?" — validating fctry's core thesis that specifications, not code, are the durable artifact (noted in `#inspirations` 5.1). CLAUDE.md under 200 lines with `.claude/rules/` decomposition was noted as a guideline consistent with fctry's existing three-layer CLAUDE.md model (evergreen + build + compact sections).

**pi-autoresearch — Deferred insights, build trace as resumption contract** (via `/fctry:ref`, 2026-03-13). Source: https://github.com/davebcn87/pi-autoresearch. An autonomous optimize-measure-keep/discard experiment loop extension for the Pi coding agent, using a living session document as the resumption surface and an append-only JSONL log for experiment tracking. Two patterns adopted into fctry: (1) Deferred insights — when the Executor discovers implementation ideas out of scope for the current chunk, it records them in the build trace under a "Deferred Insights" section; on build resume or next build, the Executor reads these and factors relevant ones into plan proposal. These are agent-discovered opportunities, distinct from user-queued inbox items (adopted into `#execute-flow` 2.7). (2) Build trace as resumption contract — the trace explicitly records tried-and-failed approaches and architectural insights alongside structural state, so a fresh Executor resuming a build knows what was attempted and why it failed, not just which chunks are done. This strengthens the trace from a progress log to a resumption contract (adopted into `#execute-flow` 2.7). Two patterns noted but not adopted: auto-resume with safeguards (pi-autoresearch automatically resumes from the living document on session restart — fctry's checkpoint resume already handles this with user confirmation at the gate), and append-only JSONL experiment log (fctry's build trace markdown format with git-tracked history serves the same role with better human readability). One pattern already handled: anti-cheating guardrail (preventing the agent from gaming optimization metrics) — fctry's Observer anti-gaming verification principle and structural Stop hook provide equivalent enforcement.

**claude-statusline — Visual dot-bar progress indicators** (via `/fctry:ref`, 2026-03-13). Source: https://github.com/kamranahmedse/claude-statusline. A community Claude Code status line that uses filled/empty dot bars (`●●●●○○○○○○`) to visualize rate limit utilization. One pattern adopted into fctry: visual dot-bar progress — the filled/empty dot pattern makes ratios immediately scannable by communicating completion through visual weight rather than requiring numeric parsing. fctry adopts this for a different purpose than claude-statusline's rate-limit display: section readiness (filled = built, empty = specced/unbuilt), chunk progress during builds (filled = completed, empty = planned), and scenario satisfaction (filled = satisfied groups, empty = unsatisfied). The dot bar appears alongside the numeric fraction so users get both the visceral scan and the precise count. Dots are grouped when totals exceed comfortable bar width to keep bars compact and comparable across different scales (adopted into `#status-line` 2.12).

**Ouroboros — Advisory interview coverage, stagnation pattern detection, knowledge gap signal, accumulated alignment** (via `/fctry:ref`, 2026-03-13). Source: https://github.com/Q00/ouroboros. A spec-first Claude Code plugin taking an ontological approach (defining project identity and boundaries through structured constraints), contrasting with fctry's experience-first methodology. Four patterns adopted into fctry: (1) Advisory interview readiness signal — the Interviewer internally tracks coverage across four experience dimensions (Vision Completeness, Flow Coverage, Edge Case Awareness, Success Definition) and steers the conversation toward uncovered areas. This is advisory, not a gate or a score — it manifests as natural interview steering behavior, preventing premature termination from conversational momentum when the user describes the happy path enthusiastically without covering error handling or success criteria (adopted into `#core-flow` 2.2). (2) Named stagnation pattern detection — three diagnostic heuristics (spinning: same error repeated; oscillation: alternating between two failing states; diminishing returns: improvement rate below threshold) that give the Executor precision when choosing which failure escalation stage to enter. These route around futile retries — spinning skips to recover, oscillation skips to restructure, diminishing returns proceeds to restructure or escalate — adding diagnostic intelligence to stage 1 of the existing four-stage escalation (adopted into `#execute-flow` 2.7). (3) Wonder signal for knowledge gap detection — after builds, the next-action recommender identifies thin spec dimensions and undertested scenarios as refinement targets, turning it from a pure task recommender into a knowledge-gap detector. Sections with rich spec text but no scenarios, or scenarios but sparse spec text, surface as `/fctry:evolve` recommendations (adopted into `#next-action` 2.13). (4) Accumulated drift signal — Observer verification verdicts contribute to a trend-aware alignment signal across the build. Sustained degradation (consecutive low-confidence passes, increasing retries, growing findings) biases the guard evaluation toward intervention rather than continuation, with the signal decaying after recovery so a rough patch doesn't permanently bias the build (adopted into `#rules` 3.3). Ouroboros' ontological constraint model (defining project identity through structured boundaries) was noted as a philosophical contrast to fctry's experience-first approach — both arrive at buildable specs through different lenses, validating spec-as-source-of-truth as a methodology-independent pattern.

**greppy — Codebase indexing tool dependency, token savings quantification, composable trace operations** (via `/fctry:ref`, 2026-03-13). Source: https://github.com/wonyoung-jang/greppy. A daemon-based codebase search engine combining BM25 full-text search with tree-sitter AST parsing, call graph tracing, and impact analysis. Sub-millisecond warm queries, concrete token benchmarks (93-98% reduction vs. raw grep/find). Three patterns adopted into fctry: (1) tool dependency addition — greppy added to the Recommended (Codebase Indexing) tool category alongside srclight and grepai, expanding the Executor's codebase orientation tool inventory (adopted into `references/tool-dependencies.md`). (2) Token savings quantification for context budget — greppy's published benchmarks (93-98% reduction with methodology) strengthened the quantified range in `#capabilities` 3.1, updating the codebase indexing token savings estimate from 40-60% to 40-98% with concrete benchmark data supporting the upper bound (adopted into `#capabilities` 3.1). (3) Composable trace operations concept — greppy's multi-analysis pipeline (chaining symbol lookup, call graph tracing, and impact analysis in a single query) informed the Executor's codebase orientation as a composable operation rather than discrete tool calls (adopted into `#capabilities` 3.1).

**sentrux — Structural regression verification, activity heat visualization, governor gap thesis** (via `/fctry:ref`, 2026-03-14). Source: https://github.com/sentrux/sentrux. A Rust-based architecture quality governance tool addressing the "governor gap" — AI generates code faster than humans can govern its structural quality. Session-scoped regression gating, 14-dimension health grading, anti-gaming composite scoring, and live interactive treemap with activity heat. Philosophical complement to fctry: fctry governs experience (spec + scenarios), sentrux governs architecture (structural metrics). Three patterns adopted into fctry: (1) Optional structural regression verification dimension — when structural analysis tools are available, the Observer can baseline architectural health before a chunk (grade, coupling trends, cycle count) and check for regression after. A chunk that satisfies scenarios but degrades structural health gets flagged as information for the Executor's guard evaluation, not as a build-stopper. This catches architectural rot that behavioral review alone cannot detect (adopted into `#execute-flow` 2.7). (2) Activity heat visualization — sections in mission control's story map glow with build activity using warm colors for currently modified sections and exponential decay for recently completed ones, giving the user spatial awareness of where the build is working without reading event logs (adopted into `#spec-viewer` 2.9). (3) Structural regression rate as an observability signal — tracking how often chunks introduce architectural degradation despite satisfying scenarios, detecting divergence between experience quality and structural quality (adopted into `#observability` 6.3). sentrux's anti-gaming composite grading (floor-mean capped by worst dimension, preventing strong dimensions from hiding weak ones) informed the structural regression dimension's design — structural health is a holistic signal, not a single metric that can be gamed.

**Symphony — Workspace reuse, turn continuation, trust boundaries, stop gate reconciliation, verification tiers, lifecycle hooks** (via `/fctry:ref`, 2026-03-13). Source: https://github.com/openai/symphony. OpenAI's spec-first autonomous coding orchestrator that polls a Linear task board, spawns isolated agent workers with persistent workspaces, manages retries with reconciliation, collects proof of work, and lands PRs. Different abstraction level (team-scale ticket-driven vs. fctry's single-user spec-driven). Six patterns adopted into fctry: (1) Workspace reuse across runs — the same issue gets the same workspace, preserving prior code artifacts for incremental work. For fctry: the build trace + git working tree together form the complete resumption context. The trace is the map (what was planned, attempted, decided); the working tree is the territory (actual code artifacts from prior chunks). A resumed build reads both, avoiding redundant re-derivation (adopted into `#execute-flow` 2.7). (2) Lightweight turn continuation — multiple agent turns within one worker use continuation guidance rather than full re-prompting on subsequent turns. For fctry: continuation between context-carrying chunks is a focused handoff (what changed, what state was left) rather than a restatement of the full plan context (adopted into `#execute-flow` 2.7). (3) Formalized trust boundary documentation — explicit trust boundary documentation with layered hardening tiers. For fctry: the first-run credential safety check establishes the project's trust boundary, and the accepted deny rules are documented in CLAUDE.md so the boundary persists across sessions (adopted into `#first-run` 2.1). (4) Reconciliation stop gates — the orchestrator continuously re-fetches external state and kills workers whose issues went terminal. For fctry: stop signals (user stop, viewer stop button, context budget gate) are reconciled at chunk boundaries — in-flight work completes its current atomic operation, then halts cleanly without starting the next chunk (adopted into `#execute-flow` 2.7). (5) Conformance test profiles — three verification tiers (core, extension, real-integration) with distinct requirements. For fctry: Observer verification has implicit tiers (mechanical, behavioral, integration) that the Observer escalates through based on chunk complexity (adopted into `#observability` 6.3). (6) Per-workspace typed lifecycle hooks — typed hooks (after_create, before_run, after_run, before_remove) with timeout enforcement. For fctry: validates the existing typed lifecycle event model as an industry pattern for agent orchestration (noted in `#observability` 6.3).

**devteam — Priority-cascade status derivation for kanban cards** (via `/fctry:ref`, 2026-03-14). Source: https://github.com/agent-era/devteam. A terminal UI for parallel coding agents in tmux sessions with git worktree isolation. One pattern adopted into fctry: priority-cascade status derivation — each kanban card derives a single canonical status from a priority waterfall (building > drift detected > untracked changes > inbox items pending > readiness label) and the most actionable dimension gets visual emphasis (color highlight) rather than showing all status dimensions equally. Within kanban columns, cards sort by urgency score so items needing attention float to the top without requiring a filter mode. This makes the kanban informationally denser: the user's eye is drawn to whichever dimension matters most right now for each card (adopted into `#spec-viewer` 2.9).

**agentops — Mandatory post-build retro, utility feedback, bidirectional maturity transitions** (via `/fctry:ref`, 2026-03-14). Source: internal assessment of build learnings feedback loop weaknesses. Three patterns adopted into fctry addressing the weakest area in build-to-build learning: (1) Mandatory post-build retro as a lifecycle phase (Step 3.5) — after all chunks complete but before the experience report, the Executor runs a structured retrospective examining what went well, what was hard, and what didn't work, extracting learnings into `lessons.md`. This is a lifecycle phase with a defined position in the build pipeline, not a discretionary Executor responsibility that can be skipped under context pressure (adopted into `#execute-flow` 2.7). (2) Utility feedback on lessons — each lesson tracks `helpful_count` and `harmful_count`. When a lesson is cited during a build and the chunk succeeds, helpful increments; when cited and the chunk fails, harmful increments. This produces a quantified utility signal beyond the existing confirmation-based confidence score (adopted into `#capabilities` 3.1 and `#entities` 3.2). (3) Bidirectional maturity transitions — promotion to `active` requires confidence >= 3 AND helpful > harmful; demotion to `anti-pattern` occurs when harmful >= 3 AND harmful > helpful. Anti-pattern lessons remain as negative examples (what NOT to do) rather than being pruned. The maturation lifecycle becomes bidirectional: lessons move from candidate → active → anti-pattern, or from active back to candidate on contradictory evidence (adopted into `#capabilities` 3.1 and `#entities` 3.2).

**opik — Multi-trial evaluation, scoring failure distinction, selective evaluation, evaluation infrastructure** (via `/fctry:ref`, 2026-03-14). Source: internal assessment of scenario evaluation mechanism weaknesses. Four patterns adopted into fctry operationalizing the existing variance acknowledgment philosophy with concrete mechanics: (1) Multi-trial evaluation — each scenario is evaluated N times (default 3), passes if M/N pass (default 2/3). The majority-vote approach absorbs LLM-as-judge noise without hiding it (adopted into `#satisfaction-definition` 6.1). (2) Scoring failure distinction — "couldn't evaluate" (LLM error, parse failure) is distinct from "not satisfied." Failed evaluations are retried once then excluded from trial counts, preventing infrastructure failures from dragging down satisfaction scores (adopted into `#satisfaction-definition` 6.1). (3) Selective per-chunk evaluation — only scenarios mapped to the chunk's target sections are evaluated per-chunk (5-15 scenarios, not all 180). Full evaluation runs at build completion and during `/fctry:review` (adopted into `#satisfaction-definition` 6.1 and `#capabilities` 3.1). (4) Evaluation as infrastructure — scenario evaluation is a dedicated subsystem with per-scenario failure isolation, not inline State Owner work. The Observer runs batch evaluations where one scenario's failure does not block the rest (adopted into `#capabilities` 3.1).

**hermes-agent-self-evolution — Causal retro analysis, growth caps, per-context ranking, scenario stratification** (via `/fctry:ref`, 2026-03-14). Source: internal assessment of feedback loop strengthening patterns. Four patterns adopted into fctry addressing gaps in how the system learns from builds and evaluates against scenarios: (1) Causal WHY-chain extraction — the mandatory retro (Step 3.5) now traces causal chains from observable failure through immediate cause to root cause, producing lessons that encode reasoning ("the component failed because the spec described an impossible state transition given the data model") rather than just outcomes ("the component failed"). This produces lessons that prevent future failures at the root, not just at the symptom (adopted into `#execute-flow` 2.7). (2) Growth cap as hard constraint — the 50-entry lessons file ceiling is now a hard cap (not a soft trigger), with bidirectional maturity and denoising as primary enforcement and compaction as safety net (adopted into `#capabilities` 3.1). (3) Per-context lesson ranking — lessons carry per-section-category and per-project-type relevance alongside global utility, preventing globally popular lessons from crowding out contextually relevant ones (adopted into `#capabilities` 3.1). (4) Scenario source stratification — scenarios can originate from three sources (hand-crafted, spec-derived, trace-mined) with the hand-crafted holdout set as the evaluation standard and supplemental sources clearly marked (adopted into `#satisfaction-definition` 6.1).

**FlowBoard — Diff-based kanban rendering, optimistic drag, wake events** (via `/fctry:ref`, 2026-03-14). Source: internal assessment of kanban interactivity patterns. Three patterns adopted into fctry transforming the kanban from a passive display to an interactive trigger surface: (1) Diff-based board rendering with interaction guard — the kanban builds its skeleton once and diffs card content in-place, with incoming updates suppressed during mid-drag or mid-input and applied as a batch when the interaction ends (adopted into `#spec-viewer` 2.9). (2) Optimistic drag-and-drop with rollback — cards move immediately in local state before server confirmation, reverting with toast on failure (adopted into `#spec-viewer` 2.9). (3) Wake events on drag — dragging a section to "Now" queues an execute action via the async inbox, making the kanban an active trigger surface (adopted into `#spec-viewer` 2.9).

**trycycle — Fresh-agent audit, convergence-failure diagnosis, independent plan validation, phase-boundary hygiene gates** (via `/fctry:ref`, 2026-03-14). Source: https://github.com/danshapiro/trycycle. An autonomous coding agent orchestration framework built around a try-verify-fix cycle with structured build phases. Four patterns adopted into fctry strengthening build verification layering and escalation precision: (1) Fresh-agent build-completion audit — after all chunks complete and the retro runs (but before the experience report), the Executor may optionally invoke a subagent with no build context that reads the full git diff against the spec sections and reports issues the Observer may have missed due to context familiarity. The fresh agent has only spec sections and the git diff — no build trace, no state.json, no conversation history. Fresh perspective catches what accumulated-context verification misses. Optional — triggered for 3+ chunk builds or when Observer confidence degraded across later chunks (adopted into `#execute-flow` 2.7). (2) Convergence-failure diagnosis — when a stagnation pattern is detected (spinning, oscillation, diminishing returns), a diagnostic pass reads the chunk's retry history, Observer verdicts, and the target spec section, then produces a root-cause hypothesis (spec ambiguity, architectural mismatch, missing dependency, environment issue) that feeds escalation routing. Adds causal precision beyond pattern detection — the diagnosis tells the Executor not just that retrying is futile, but why, and which escalation stage will actually help (adopted into `#execute-flow` 2.7). (3) Independent plan validation — after the user approves the plan but before execution begins, a fresh agent reviews the approved plan against the spec and scenarios for gaps, unrealistic scope, and missing dependencies. The validator has only the spec, scenarios, and the plan — no briefing, no conversation context. Plan-level issues are cheap to fix and expensive to discover mid-build. Optional — triggered for 4+ chunk plans or on user request (adopted into `#execute-flow` 2.7). (4) Phase-boundary hygiene gates — at each phase boundary (plan-to-first-chunk, between chunks, last-chunk-to-retro), the Executor verifies ground-truth conditions: git status matches expectations, build checkpoint is internally consistent, spec hasn't changed for in-progress sections. Lightweight (file reads and state comparisons, not a full scan). Complements the guard evaluation's progress quality assessment with state consistency verification — catches drift between what agents report and actual repository state (adopted into `#execute-flow` 2.7).

**sem — Entity-level codebase analysis for verification and chunk sizing** (via `/fctry:ref`, 2026-03-14). Source: internal assessment of entity-level analysis patterns. Three patterns adopted into fctry, all strengthening the Observer's verification precision and the Executor's chunk planning when codebase indexing tools are available: (1) Entity-level change summaries — the Observer's scope compliance check reasons about which functions, classes, and exports changed rather than just which files were modified. Entity-level summaries produce 2.3x higher agent accuracy on code understanding tasks vs. raw file diffs, making verification findings more precise (adopted into `#execute-flow` 2.7 and `#capabilities` 3.1). (2) Structural-vs-cosmetic distinction — when verifying scope compliance, the Observer distinguishes entities with structural (logic) changes from cosmetic (formatting) changes. A chunk touching 50 entities with 3 structural changes is fundamentally different from one with 50 structural changes, and the verification response should be proportional (adopted into `#execute-flow` 2.7). (3) Entity-level impact for chunk sizing — during plan proposal, entity-level dependency awareness informs chunk boundaries. "Changing this function impacts 12 callers across 3 packages" means a larger chunk than line-count suggests (adopted into `#execute-flow` 2.7).

**hive — Structured escalation, doom-loop detection, and episodic session journals** (via `/fctry:ref`, 2026-03-14). Source: internal assessment of escalation and detection patterns. Three patterns adopted into fctry, strengthening build monitoring and cross-session continuity: (1) Structured escalation tickets — when the guard evaluation routes to "intervene" or "escalate," it emits a structured escalation document with severity, cause, evidence snippet, and suggested action. The viewer renders these as dismissable cards in mission control, giving the user a clear picture of what went wrong and what to do about it (adopted into `#execute-flow` 2.7 and `#spec-viewer` 2.9). (2) Doom-loop detection — a PostToolUse hook or instruction-level check detects when the Executor is calling the same tools with identical arguments in a tight loop. This is a distinct failure mode from build-level stagnation (spinning, oscillation, diminishing returns): stagnation is spinning at the chunk level, doom-loops are spinning at the tool-call level. Detection routes to the next escalation stage (adopted into `#execute-flow` 2.7). (3) Episodic session journals — per-session records of what happened (distinct from lessons, which capture what was learned, and build traces, which capture build execution). A future direction that would make State Owner briefings richer over time by providing continuity across non-build sessions (adopted into `#execute-flow` 2.7 as future direction).

**Dolt — Content-addressable sections, blame metadata, change-type taxonomy, three-way drift model** (via `/fctry:ref`, 2026-03-14). Source: https://github.com/dolthub/dolt. A SQL database with git-like version control — branch, merge, diff, blame, and bisect for structured data. A cross-domain transfer: Dolt solves version control for database rows, fctry solves version control for spec sections. Five patterns adopted into fctry, all strengthening the spec index and drift detection infrastructure: (1) Content hash per section — the spec index computes and stores a content hash for each section during the existing parse step, enabling O(1) change detection without reading section content. Powers drift detection (hash mismatch = drift), changelog accuracy (only log sections whose hash changed), viewer diff rendering (highlight changed sections), and the State Owner's freshness-skip filter (adopted into `#entities` 3.2 and `#capabilities` 3.1). (2) Blame-like metadata per section — each section record carries `last_changed_version`, `last_changed_command`, and `last_changed_at`, answering "which command last touched Core Flow, when, at what version?" without git archaeology. The viewer's section detail surfaces this directly (adopted into `#entities` 3.2). (3) Change-type taxonomy in changelog — each changelog entry carries a per-section change type (added, removed, modified, structural) plus aggregate stats, making the changelog machine-parseable for the viewer and State Owner (adopted into `#capabilities` 3.1). (4) Three-way drift model — drift detection compares ancestor (last aligned version) vs. spec vs. code, classifying drift as spec-ahead, code-ahead, or diverged. Distinguishes "spec evolved and code didn't follow" from "code evolved and spec doesn't reflect it" from "both changed independently" (adopted into `#review-flow` 2.6). (5) Structured drift persistence — drift entries stored as structured data in state.json with section, specSays, codeSays, classification, detectedAtVersion, and detectedAt fields. The viewer renders a persistent drift list that survives conversation context (adopted into `#review-flow` 2.6).

---

## 6. Satisfaction and Convergence

### 6.1 Satisfaction Definition {#satisfaction-definition}

The fctry system is satisfactory when:

- A non-coder with a project idea can run `/fctry:init`, describe their vision conversationally, and receive a complete, coherent spec within 20 minutes — without needing to understand databases, APIs, or architecture.

- The user can pause an interview, return days later, and resume exactly where they left off with no loss of context or repeated questions.

- The user can point to any part of the spec (by number or alias) and update it in isolation, seeing exactly what changed and confident that unrelated sections remain untouched.

- When the spec and code diverge, the system surfaces the conflict with specific evidence (recency, changelog, code behavior) and asks the user to resolve it, never guessing or assuming.

- The user can incorporate external inspiration (a URL, a screenshot, a design) and see it interpreted in experience language and integrated into the spec within 60 seconds.

- The user can run `/fctry:execute`, approve a build plan, and watch the system build autonomously toward scenario satisfaction — with ambient progress visibility through mission control and the status line, convergence milestones at phase boundaries, and the ability to resume interrupted builds where they left off.

- The user can open the spec viewer and see the spec rendered cleanly, watch it update in real-time as agents work, navigate the change history, and understand exactly what's changing and why.

- The user feels like they have a co-founder with perfect memory, deep context, and the ability to translate their vision into a buildable spec without them needing to learn to code.

**Multi-trial evaluation.** Scenario satisfaction is evaluated by LLM-as-judge, which is inherently noisy — the same implementation evaluated twice can produce different satisfaction results. To operationalize this variance honestly, each scenario is evaluated N times (default 3) and passes if M/N trials pass (default 2/3). This majority-vote approach absorbs evaluation noise without hiding it: a scenario that passes 2/3 is satisfied, one that passes 1/3 is not, and one that passes 3/3 is confidently satisfied. The trial count and pass threshold are configurable per-project in `.fctry/config.json` under `evaluation.trials` and `evaluation.passThreshold`. **Scoring failure distinction:** "couldn't evaluate" (LLM error, parse failure, tool timeout) is distinct from "not satisfied." Failed evaluations are retried once, then excluded from the trial count — a scenario with 2 successful evaluations (both passing) and 1 failed evaluation is 2/2, not 2/3. This prevents infrastructure failures from dragging down satisfaction scores. **Cross-model distribution:** when multi-model assessment adapters are available (see `#capabilities` 3.1), the multi-trial evaluation can distribute trials across models (e.g., 2 Claude + 1 Gemini out of 3 trials). Cross-model agreement strengthens confidence; cross-model disagreement flags ambiguity for spec clarification or human review. **Selective evaluation strategy:** per-chunk evaluation covers only scenarios affected by the chunk's target sections (matched via the scenario-to-section mappings in the spec index), keeping evaluation fast and focused. Full evaluation (all scenarios) runs at build completion and during `/fctry:review`. **Evaluation as infrastructure:** scenario evaluation is a dedicated subsystem, not inline State Owner work. The Observer runs batch evaluations with failure isolation — one scenario's evaluation failure does not block the other evaluations. Results are collected, aggregated, and written to the build run state as a batch, with per-scenario verdicts available for inspection in mission control and the experience report.

**Scenario source stratification.** Scenarios can be augmented from three sources: **hand-crafted** (authored by the Scenario Crafter, the primary source — these form the holdout set and are the evaluation standard), **spec-derived** (auto-generated from section descriptions — lightweight scenarios that verify each section's described behaviors exist, useful for coverage breadth but lower fidelity than hand-crafted), and **trace-mined** (derived from actual build failures — when the retro identifies a failure pattern that no existing scenario covers, the Scenario Crafter can craft a scenario from the trace to prevent regression). The hand-crafted holdout set remains the authoritative evaluation standard. Spec-derived and trace-mined scenarios supplement it for coverage but are clearly marked with their source type so the evaluation distinguishes between holdout satisfaction (the primary signal) and supplemental coverage (additional confidence).

**Evaluation variance acknowledgment.** The multi-trial approach acknowledges that experience-level quality is genuinely ambiguous, and a probabilistic signal is the honest representation. The system treats satisfaction as a trend (improving, stable, declining) rather than a precise measurement. When a scenario flips between satisfied and unsatisfied across evaluations, the system notes the instability rather than treating either evaluation as definitive. The trial count creates a quantified instability signal: a scenario that passes 1/3, then 2/3, then 3/3 across successive builds is converging; one that stays at 1/3 or 2/3 across builds is stuck.

### 6.2 Convergence Strategy {#convergence-strategy}

**Start with:** Core command loop and multi-session interviews.

The first working version demonstrates `/fctry:init` with conversational interviewing, state persistence (save and pause, resume), State Owner briefing, Scenario Crafter and Spec Writer producing the spec and scenarios, and addressable sections (aliases and numbers). The user can create a complete spec, stop partway, and resume later.

**Then layer in:** Evolve, ref, and review commands.

Once init works, add `/fctry:evolve <section>` with targeted interviews, diff summaries, and conflict resolution (spec vs. code drift detection). Add `/fctry:ref <url>` with Researcher and Visual Translator, experience-language interpretation, and both targeted and open modes. Add `/fctry:review` with gap analysis and recommendations.

**Next:** Execute with plan-gated autonomous building.

Add `/fctry:execute` with the Executor agent, scenario satisfaction evaluation, build plan proposal and approval, autonomous chunked execution with execution priorities shaping parallelization and failure behavior, and build checkpointing so interrupted builds can resume where they left off.

**Then:** Tool validation and changelog integration.

Add startup tool validation (check for rg, sg, gh, MCP servers) with fail-fast errors and installation instructions. Add changelog maintenance (append-only, timestamped, machine-readable). Wire the State Owner to read the changelog for sharper drift detection and trajectory analysis.

**Then:** Live spec viewer.

Add the local web UI (started via `/fctry:view`, stopped via `/fctry:stop`) with zero-build markdown rendering, WebSocket-based real-time updates, section highlighting when agents are working, change history timeline with diffs, and keyboard navigation. The user can watch the spec evolve in real-time.

**Then:** Autonomous execution.

Evolve execute from paced (per-chunk approval) to autonomous (plan-level approval). Add experience reports (not satisfaction scorecards) and silent failure handling. The build plan shows execution order and dependency handling. The system resurfaces only for experience-level questions.

**Then:** Viewer mission control and async inbox.

Transform the viewer into mission control during builds (real-time concurrent progress, section lighting, dependency visualization). Add the async inbox (evolve ideas, reference URLs, new features) that processes in the background. The factory never idles — builds, reference analysis, and evolve prep all run concurrently.

**Then:** Multi-project viewer.

Evolve the viewer from per-project servers to a single multi-project-aware server. Add the global project registry (`~/.fctry/projects.json`), auto-registration on init, project sidebar with quick status, and full context switching between projects. The server persists across sessions and self-heals.

**Then:** Kanban as primary interface.

Replace the static project dashboard with a recursive kanban board. Projects become draggable cards in priority columns (Inbox/Now/Next/Later/Satisfied). Clicking a project drills into a section-level kanban with a section/scenario toggle. Clicking a section drills into claim-level cards. Inbox items land as inbox cards. Kanban position drives State Owner assessment depth, Executor build ordering, and review granularity. The kanban is the primary observation, decision-making, and prioritization surface — the user looks, prioritizes, and decides here, Claude Code executes.

**Then:** Next-action recommender.

Add `/fctry:next` — a lightweight command that reads cached state (state.json, inbox.json) without a full codebase scan, ranks possible next actions by priority (incomplete builds, untracked changes, inbox items, ready-to-build sections, convergence order), presents a recommendation with rationale, and chains into the recommended command on confirmation. The user always knows what to do next without waiting for a deep scan.

**Then:** Automatic diagramming and visual polish.

Add five types of auto-generated Mermaid diagrams (entity relationships, user flows, agent pipeline, convergence phases, section dependency neighborhoods). Per-section toggle with `d` shortcut and global "show all as diagrams" mode. Add dark mode (system-detected with manual override, Radix-based token system). Add visual polish: skeleton loading, syntax highlighting, fuzzy search, styled toasts, uncapped activity feed.

**Finally:** Viewer as control plane.

Embed a terminal emulator (e.g., xterm.js + node-pty) in the viewer, running Claude Code inside the browser. This inverts the current model — the viewer becomes the primary surface where the user thinks, plans, and directs work, while Claude Code runs as the execution engine within it. The user sees all their projects, switches between them, kicks off builds, has conversations, and watches mission control — all in one window. This is the long-term architectural direction: the viewer wraps around Claude Code rather than sitting alongside it. **Parity principle:** as the viewer gains interactive capabilities, anything the user can do via the CLI should be achievable via the viewer — and vice versa. The viewer is not a read-only dashboard with a CLI escape hatch; it's a full alternative surface. This means `/fctry:evolve`, `/fctry:ref`, and `/fctry:execute` should all be invocable from the viewer, with results flowing back through the same interchange and WebSocket channels that already render agent outputs. **Session resume from viewer.** The viewer can detect the user's terminal application (iTerm2, Terminal.app, Kitty) and launch a `claude --resume` session directly from the browser UI, enabling the user to resume a prior Claude Code session from the viewer without switching to the terminal first. Combined with MCP elicitation, this means the viewer can both ask questions and launch sessions — a concrete step toward viewer-as-control-plane.

**MCP elicitation as the platform mechanism.** MCP elicitation — the platform capability allowing MCP server tools to prompt users for input directly during tool execution — is the concrete mechanism that makes viewer-as-control-plane achievable without embedding a terminal. If the viewer exposes an MCP server, its tools can elicit user input as part of their execution: `approve_chunk` presents a plan chunk for approval, `answer_experience_question` surfaces a build question with options, `prioritize_section` lets the user reorder kanban priorities, `submit_evolve_idea` accepts an evolve idea with section targeting. This replaces the previously-considered HITL WebSocket callback pattern with a first-class platform primitive — the viewer's MCP tools participate in the conversation loop natively rather than requiring custom IPC between the viewer and Claude Code.

**Convergence phases as version arcs.** Each convergence phase described above maps to a minor version arc: completing the core command loop is a minor bump, completing the viewer is a minor bump, completing mission control is a minor bump. When the Executor suggests a minor version bump at the end of a build plan, it characterizes the phase as an experience era in the release notes — not just listing what changed, but describing the experience shift the phase represents ("the viewer era is complete — you can now watch every spec update and build in real-time from the browser"). This makes version numbers meaningful: v0.2.0 → v0.3.0 tells a story, not just a changelog. The phase type inferred for each build plan (see `#rules` 3.3) shapes this narrative: a Capability phase completion reads differently from a Hardening phase completion. Major version bumps mark experience eras that span multiple phases — the transition from "spec authoring only" to "autonomous builds live" is a major bump regardless of which phase technically completed it.

**Future direction: Incremental build trace parsing.** Build traces (`.fctry/build-trace-{runId}.md`) are currently written as a complete document when the build completes. An incremental append-only format — where each chunk completion appends a structured entry to the trace file during the build rather than constructing the full trace at the end — would enable the viewer to stream trace data in real-time and allow the State Owner to read partial traces from interrupted builds. This pattern (from claude-devtools' incremental log parsing) is noted here for future implementation when build traces become a first-class live data source rather than a post-build artifact.

**Future direction: Self-improvement loop.** A `/fctry:reflect` command that analyzes recent session behavior — retry rates, token patterns, drift frequency, friction points — and proposes config, prompt, or hook improvements. The system observes its own execution patterns and suggests how to improve itself. Rather than requiring the user to tune the system when something feels slow or error-prone, the reflect command surfaces specific proposals: "your builds average 2.3 retries per chunk across the last 5 sessions — these sections tend to generate experience questions: consider evolving `#core-flow` to be more specific." This extends fctry from a user-directed tool to a self-improving system. The reflect loop would use the same findings/proposals pattern as `/fctry:review` but turned inward — examining fctry's own behavior rather than spec-code alignment. Proposals are numbered and require user approval before any config changes are made.

**Future direction: Experimental autonomous loop as convergence endgame.** The experimental fully-autonomous loop (see `#capabilities` 3.1) represents the logical endpoint of the convergence strategy. When all phases above are built and the feedback loop is working — gap detection finds weak areas, source discovery finds inspiration, the Researcher evaluates it, the Spec Writer incorporates it, the Executor builds it, the Observer verifies it, and the retro extracts lessons — the entire cycle can run without human direction. The system autonomously discovers its own weaknesses, finds relevant prior art, incorporates improvements into the spec, builds from the updated spec, verifies the results, and learns from the experience. The human's role shifts from directing each step to reviewing autonomous results: returning to a worktree with N cycles of improvement, merging what's good, discarding what's not. This is not a replacement for human vision — the user still sets the direction via the spec and scenarios — but it is an amplifier: the system does the research, synthesis, and building between sessions, producing concrete results for the user to evaluate rather than requiring the user to initiate every action. Worktree isolation makes this safe to experiment with — the worst outcome is a deleted worktree, not a corrupted project.

### 6.3 Observability {#observability}

Key signals to watch:

- **Interview completion rate.** What fraction of users who start `/fctry:init` complete the interview vs. abandon partway? High abandonment suggests the interview is too long, too tedious, or not drawing out the vision effectively.

- **Multi-session usage.** How often do users pause and resume interviews? If rarely, multi-session support may not be needed. If frequently, it's critical — and we should watch how long between pause and resume (hours? days? weeks?).

- **Section update frequency.** Which sections get updated most often via `/fctry:evolve`? High churn in a section suggests it's under-specified or the user's vision is still forming. Low churn suggests the spec is stable.

- **Drift detection accuracy.** When the State Owner flags drift, how often does the user confirm it's real vs. dismiss it as a false positive? High false positive rate suggests the drift detection logic needs refinement.

- **Execute chunk success rate.** What fraction of build chunks succeed on first attempt vs. require retry or manual intervention? High retry rate suggests the spec is ambiguous or the coding agent is misinterpreting it.

- **Scenario satisfaction trajectory.** How quickly does satisfaction improve during `/fctry:execute`? Steady improvement suggests the build is converging. Flat or declining satisfaction suggests the spec and scenarios are misaligned or the coding agent is stuck.

- **Execution priority distribution.** What rankings do users choose? If most users rank speed first, the default preset should favor speed. If most use the same ranking across all projects, per-project overrides may be unnecessary complexity. If rankings vary significantly by project, per-project support is justified.

- **Spec viewer usage.** Do users keep the viewer open while running commands? Do they interact with the change history? If the viewer is rarely used, it may not be delivering enough value to justify the complexity.

- **Tool validation failure rate.** How often do users hit missing tool errors? If frequently, the installation instructions need to be clearer or the tool dependency list needs to shrink.

- **Time to first spec.** How long does it take from `/fctry:init` to a complete, satisfactory spec? Target: under 20 minutes for a simple project. If consistently over 30 minutes, the interview is too long or the Spec Writer is too slow.

- **Workflow enforcement trigger rate.** How often do agents hit the "State Owner must run first" error? High rates suggest the enforcement is catching real process drift. If it never fires, the enforcement may be unnecessary overhead — or the process is being followed naturally.

- **Verifiable build artifacts.** Build traces (`.fctry/build-trace-{runId}.md`) include executable proof blocks alongside their narrative content — shell commands with captured expected output that can be re-run to confirm the build is reproducible. When the Observer verifies a chunk, the verification commands and their results are embedded as fenced code blocks marked with an `exec` annotation. A future `showboat verify` integration (or equivalent) could re-execute these blocks and diff against recorded outputs, turning the build trace from a human-readable narrative into a machine-verifiable proof of work. This strengthens confidence in autonomous builds: the trace doesn't just describe what happened — it proves it can happen again.

- **Untracked change frequency.** How often does the PostToolUse hook detect file writes outside fctry commands that cover spec sections? High frequency suggests users are doing ad-hoc development between fctry commands — the nudge may need to be less intrusive, or `/fctry:review` needs to better reconcile untracked changes.

- **Section readiness distribution.** What fraction of sections are in each readiness state (draft, undocumented, ready-to-build, aligned, ready-to-execute, satisfied, deferred)? A project with many `undocumented` sections has code outpacing the spec. A project with many `ready-to-build` sections has a detailed spec but little implementation.

- **Spec index rebuild frequency.** How often does the SQLite cache rebuild from the markdown? Frequent rebuilds (every few seconds) during active evolve sessions are expected. Rebuilds outside of fctry commands suggest the user is editing the spec manually — which should be rare.

- **Chunk retry rate.** How often do chunks need multiple attempts, and how many attempts are typical? A high retry rate across builds suggests specs are consistently ambiguous or the coding agent is struggling with certain patterns. A low rate with occasional spikes points to specific section complexity. Tracked per build run.

- **Build run duration.** Wall-clock time from plan approval to experience report. Compared against the plan's estimated time to calibrate future estimates. Tracked per build run alongside chunk count and parallelism level.

- **Build autonomy distribution.** Per-build breakdown of what percentage of actions were agent-initiated vs. human-initiated. A build with 95% autonomy validates the factory model; a build with 40% autonomy suggests the spec needs more detail or the plan had too many ambiguities. Shown in the viewer's build history alongside duration and chunk count to help calibrate spec quality over time.

- **Chunk throughput metrics.** Time per chunk, tool calls per chunk, and sections touched per chunk, broken down by chunk complexity (small/medium/large). Velocity data helps the user calibrate expectations ("5-chunk builds take about 45 minutes at this complexity level") and identifies chunks that are disproportionately slow relative to their estimated complexity.

- **External tool call frequency.** How often do agents invoke MCP tools (Firecrawl, Context7, Playwright) during builds? Which tools are used most? If a build involves many external calls, the activity feed becomes richer and more informative. If certain tools fail frequently, the user should know (via the experience report) that external dependencies slowed the build.

- **Build log export usage.** How often do users download build logs after completion? If rarely, the export feature may not justify its placement. If frequently, it validates that users want a record of what the system did.

- **Build resume frequency.** How often do builds get interrupted and resumed? If rarely (most builds complete in one session), the checkpoint system may be over-engineered. If frequently, it validates the investment. Track reasons for interruption: session crash, context exhaustion, user closed laptop, explicit stop at milestone.

- **Convergence milestone interaction.** When milestones are presented, how often do users try the system at that point? How often do they stop the build at a milestone vs. letting it continue? High stop rates at milestones suggest users are finding problems early — which is the point. If milestones are always ignored, they may be too noisy.

- **Build coordination effectiveness.** How often does the Executor detect and recover from stuck chunks, and what's the recovery success rate? If the coordinator rarely intervenes, builds may be simple enough that coordination adds no value. If it intervenes often and succeeds, the pattern is justified.

- **Observer verification pass rate.** What fraction of post-chunk verifications pass on first attempt? A consistently high pass rate (95%+) suggests the build is reliable and verification is confirming quality. A low pass rate suggests either the Observer's checks are too strict or the build quality needs attention. Track pass-on-first-attempt vs. pass-on-retry vs. failed-after-retry to understand transient vs. persistent issues.

- **Verification tier distribution.** Observer verification has implicit tiers — mechanical (files exist, code runs, scope compliance), behavioral (output matches spec, interaction patterns correct), and integration (end-to-end flow works across sections). The Observer naturally escalates through tiers based on chunk complexity: trivial chunks get mechanical checks, structural chunks get behavioral review, cross-section chunks get integration verification. Track which tier is used per chunk to understand whether the Observer's tier selection correlates with chunk complexity — consistently over-verifying simple chunks wastes tokens, consistently under-verifying complex chunks misses issues.

- **Interview lifecycle events.** InterviewStarted/Completed events so the viewer can show interview progress during `/fctry:init` and `/fctry:evolve`. CheckpointSaved events emitted when build state is persisted, improving viewer awareness of build resumability. These complement the existing build lifecycle events (chunk-started, completed, failed, retrying) with coverage for non-build workflows. The typed, per-chunk lifecycle event model (with distinct event types for each lifecycle transition) is an industry pattern for agent orchestration — validated by external systems that use timeout-enforced lifecycle hooks at workspace boundaries.

- **Observer degradation frequency.** How often does the Observer operate in reduced mode (API + files only) or minimal mode (files only) instead of system-wide mode (Peekaboo + browser + API + files) or full mode (browser + API + files)? Frequent degradation suggests browser or system-wide tools aren't reliably available — the system still works, but verification coverage is reduced. If degradation is rare, the full tooling stack is healthy.

- **Application-level verification coverage.** When Peekaboo is available, what fraction of build chunks trigger non-browser verification (native app windows, terminal UIs, system dialogs)? High usage validates the investment in system-wide observation. Low usage suggests most builds are web-only and browser tools suffice.

- **Screenshot capture surface distribution.** Of all Observer screenshots taken during builds, what fraction come from browser tools (Rodney/Surf) vs. system-wide tools (Peekaboo)? A shift toward system-wide captures over time suggests builds are producing more diverse output surfaces.

- **Average checks per chunk.** How many Observer checks does a typical chunk trigger? Too few checks suggest verification is shallow; too many suggest it's over-thorough and adding latency without proportional value. Track alongside chunk complexity (number of affected sections, estimated time).

- **Screenshot capture success rate.** When the Observer attempts to take a screenshot (for evidence or verification), how often does it succeed? Screenshot failures typically indicate tool availability issues. High success rates validate the Rodney/Surf/Peekaboo integration; low rates suggest the system should rely more on API-based checks. Peekaboo captures cover non-browser surfaces (native app windows, terminal UIs, system dialogs) where Rodney and Surf cannot reach.

- **Context compaction frequency during builds.** How often does auto-compaction fire during build sessions? If frequently, builds are pushing context limits and the Executor's context boundary strategy is load-bearing. If rarely, most builds fit comfortably in the context window and context management is low-value overhead. Track alongside build size (chunk count, total estimated time) to understand which builds stress context.

- **Verification guideline suppression rate.** When project-scoped verification guidelines are declared (see `#capabilities` 3.1), how often do they suppress findings that would otherwise have been flagged? A high suppression rate validates the guidelines. A low rate suggests the guidelines are redundant or the Observer isn't flagging those patterns anyway.

- **Build quality consistency across chunks.** Is there measurable quality degradation in later chunks compared to earlier ones (more retries, lower scenario satisfaction rates, more Observer verification failures)? If later chunks consistently perform worse, context pressure may be a factor even when the system doesn't explicitly compact. If quality is consistent, the context management strategy is working.

- **Retrospective context efficiency in experience reports.** After a build completes, the experience report includes a brief context health summary: whether compaction fired, how many times, which sections drove the most context pressure, and whether context fidelity settings were adequate. This is retrospective information reviewed at the user's discretion — not a real-time alert during the build. A build that completed with no compaction gets a single line ("context pressure: none"). A build with frequent compaction gets a breakdown showing which chunks stressed context and what fidelity mode was used.

- **Structural regression rate.** When structural analysis tools are available, how often do chunks introduce architectural degradation (new dependency cycles, coupling increases, grade drops) despite satisfying scenarios? A high structural regression rate suggests the build is trading long-term maintainability for short-term satisfaction — the experience looks right but the codebase is becoming harder to change. Track alongside scenario satisfaction trajectory to detect divergence between experience quality and structural quality.

- **Verification debt accumulation rate.** How many findings recur across successive builds? A rising verification debt backlog suggests the system is generating new code without resolving existing quality issues. A declining backlog suggests builds are genuinely improving quality. Track debt items per section alias to identify sections that chronically accumulate unresolved findings — these may need spec clarification rather than more build attempts.

- **Agent behavioral compliance.** Do agents follow their declared constraints under pressure — token economy rules, interchange emission, experience-language-only output? Behavioral tests (e.g., "given a 50-section spec, does the State Owner's briefing stay under the prose budget?", "does the Executor emit a valid interchange document after each chunk?") catch constraint drift that functional tests miss. Track compliance rates per agent per constraint category to identify which rules agents reliably follow vs. which need stronger enforcement or simpler formulation. The rationalization-resistant instruction design methodology (see `#rules` 3.3) is the authoring practice that this signal validates — when compliance drops for a specific constraint, the TDD-for-instructions cycle (pressure test, write instruction, close loopholes) produces the fix. Common rationalization patterns ("too simple for the full process," "already know enough to skip") are documented in agent instruction files as explicit counter-arguments, so the system's response to non-compliance is pre-written rather than improvised.

### 6.4 What the Agent Decides {#agent-decides}

The coding agent has full authority over:

- Technology choices (language, framework, database, tooling) — The spec describes experience; the agent picks the stack that best delivers it.
- Architecture and code structure — Monolith, microservices, layered, hexagonal — all agent decisions.
- Data model design — Tables, schemas, indexes, relations — inferred from the entities described in section 3.2, but the agent designs the actual data model.
- Internal APIs and interfaces — How components talk to each other, what data they exchange, what contracts they expose — all agent decisions.
- Testing strategy and tooling — Unit tests, integration tests, E2E tests (beyond the scenarios) — agent's choice.
- Build and deployment configuration — How the code is built, bundled, and run — agent's choice.
- Error handling implementation — How errors are caught, logged, and recovered from — agent's choice, constrained only by the user experience in section 2.10.
- Performance optimization approach — Caching, indexing, lazy loading, batching — agent's choice, constrained only by the performance expectations in section 3.5.
- Agent implementation (prompts, orchestration logic, file I/O, state management) — The coding agent that builds fctry itself decides how the agents are implemented, how they communicate, and how state is persisted. Native subagent capabilities — model selection (`model: haiku/sonnet/opus`), permission modes (`permissionMode`), turn limits (`maxTurns`), isolation levels (`isolation: worktree`), and agent-scoped memory (`memory:`) — are platform features available for agent implementation decisions.
- MCP server implementation (WebSocket protocol, markdown rendering, change history storage) — The coding agent decides how the spec viewer is built and served.
- Execution strategy — How chunks are ordered and executed, including whether to attempt any form of parallelism (e.g., subagents for independent chunks) or run purely sequentially. Today, builds run sequentially in dependency order; the agent may evolve toward parallelism as tooling matures. The agent's strategy is guided by the user's execution priorities: speed-first priorities favor aggressive retries and moving past failures, reliability-first priorities favor conservative approaches with thorough verification, token-efficiency-first priorities favor context reuse and minimal overhead.
- Context fidelity between chunks — How much context from a completed chunk carries into a dependent chunk: full transcript, trimmed transcript (full conversation with tool result bodies stubbed — preserves reasoning chain while reclaiming 50-70% of token budget), structured summary, or fresh start with artifacts only. Guided by execution priorities: token-efficiency-first favors trimmed transcript or minimal context, reliability-first favors full or trimmed transcript, speed-first favors compact summaries.
- Build coordination strategy — How to monitor build health, detect stuck chunks, and rebalance work across the dependency graph. The agent decides when a chunk is stuck (vs. just slow), when to escalate to an experience question (vs. trying another approach), and how to reorder remaining work when the graph allows it.
- Async inbox processing — How the viewer inbox items are queued, processed, and stored. The spec describes the three item types and their expected outcomes; the agent decides the processing pipeline.
- Observer verification depth — How many checks to run per chunk, whether to use browser tools or API-only inspection, and how thorough to make each check. The spec describes the Observer's capabilities and degradation levels; the agent decides the verification strategy for each chunk based on execution priorities and tool availability. Reliability-first priorities favor thorough browser-based verification; speed-first priorities favor lightweight API checks; token-efficiency-first priorities favor minimal file-only inspection.
- Context isolation between chunks — Whether to use subagent boundaries, fresh sessions, or other mechanisms to ensure each chunk operates with clean context. The spec requires that context pressure never degrades build quality; the agent decides the isolation mechanism. Guided by execution priorities: reliability-first favors strict isolation (each chunk in a fresh context), speed-first favors lightweight boundaries (summaries between chunks), token-efficiency-first favors context reuse where safe.

The agent's implementation decisions are constrained only by:

- The design principles in section 1.3 (experience language, agent decides, grounded in reality, approval-gated, conversational, progressive, addressable)
- The hard constraints in section 4.4 (Claude Code plugin model, experience language only, scenario holdout separation, approval-gated execution, State Owner first, no code review)
- The experience described in section 2 (what the user sees, does, and feels at every step)
- Satisfaction of the scenarios in `.fctry/scenarios.md` (the holdout set)
- The user's execution priorities (speed, token efficiency, reliability ranking) — for failure behavior, retry strategy, verification depth, and context management decisions

No human reviews the code. The code is validated solely through scenario satisfaction and convergence.

---

## Appendix A: Decision Rationale

**Why experience language only, never implementation language?**

The target user is a non-coder with a clear vision but no mental model of databases, APIs, or architecture. If the spec uses technical language, the user can't meaningfully review or refine it. Experience language ("the user sees a list sorted by urgency") is accessible, reviewable, and precise enough for a coding agent to infer implementation. This decision rules out any UI that asks the user about technical details.

**Why are scenarios in a separate file from the spec?**

In machine learning, holdout sets are kept separate from training data to prevent overfitting. The same principle applies here: if scenarios are visible to the coding agent during development, the agent can "teach to the test" by implementing exactly what the scenarios check and nothing more. Separation ensures the agent builds from the spec (the experience) and scenarios validate the result, not guide it.

**Why must the State Owner always run first?**

Without grounding in reality, spec updates become speculative. The State Owner provides the current state: what code exists, what the spec says, where they diverge, what changed recently. Every other agent operates on this briefing. If the State Owner runs later (or not at all), agents work from assumptions, leading to specs that describe things that don't exist or ignore things that do.

**Why addressable sections with both aliases and numbers?**

Humans remember concepts, not numbers. "The core flow" is easier to recall than "section 2.2." But numbers provide stable, unambiguous references that survive renames. Supporting both gives users the flexibility to reference sections however they think about them, without the system needing to guess or resolve ambiguity.

**Why plan-level approval instead of per-chunk gates?**

The user needs to control the scope and direction of builds, but not the moment-to-moment execution. Plan approval gives the user authority over what gets built, how much work is in scope, and the execution strategy. Per-chunk gates added friction ("chunk 1 done, continue?") without adding value — the user always said yes. After plan approval, the system executes autonomously, handling technical decisions silently. The user retains control through the plan itself and through experience questions when the spec is ambiguous.

**Why multi-session interviews?**

Real projects are complex. Users need time to think, gather information, or consult others before answering some questions. Forcing completion in one session leads to shallow specs or abandoned interviews. Multi-session support respects the user's time and thinking process, making spec authoring a progressive activity rather than a marathon session.

**Why enforce the workflow instead of just documenting it?**

Documentation describes intent; enforcement ensures adherence. When the process is only documented, Claude can (and does) skip steps — going straight to code when a quick fix seems obvious, bypassing the State Owner scan, updating code without updating the spec. For a non-coder, this is invisible: they think they're working within the factory model, but they're actually in ad-hoc mode. Enforcement makes the boundary explicit. The numbered-options error ("Run State Owner now / Skip / Abort") keeps it conversational rather than rigid — the user can always skip, but they do so consciously.

**Why SQLite as a cache instead of as the primary store?**

The markdown spec must remain portable and human-readable. A non-coder should be able to open the spec in any text editor or viewer. Making SQLite the primary store would lock the spec behind a database that requires tooling to read. By keeping markdown as source of truth and SQLite as a derived cache, the system gets structured queries (section-level access, cross-references, readiness filtering) without sacrificing portability. If the database file is deleted, corrupted, or missing, agents fall back to reading the full markdown file — the system degrades gracefully rather than failing.

**Why detect untracked changes via a hook instead of only during review?**

Non-coders may not realize that fixing a bug directly in code creates drift between the spec and the implementation. By the time they run `/fctry:review`, the drift may have compounded across multiple files. Real-time detection surfaces the issue immediately with a gentle nudge — not a blocking error. The user can dismiss it and reconcile later, but they're aware. This is designed to be dialed back: if the nudge proves too intrusive, it can be moved to `/fctry:review`-only detection without changing the spec.

**Why autonomous execution after plan approval instead of per-chunk approval gates?**

Per-chunk gates were designed to prevent unbounded resource consumption, but in practice they interrupt the user for decisions that don't require human judgment — "chunk 1 done, continue to chunk 2?" The user always says yes. The meaningful decision is the plan itself: what scope of work, which scenarios, what parallelization strategy. Once that's approved, the remaining gates add friction without adding value. The user still controls scope (through the plan) and can still observe progress (through mission control). They lose the ability to pause mid-build, but gain uninterrupted autonomous progress. Code failures, retries, and rearchitecting are the agent's domain — surfacing them breaks the factory model's core promise that no human touches or reviews the code.

**Why does the agent resurface only for experience questions?**

The factory line is clear: human and LLM collaborate on vision (init, evolve, ref, review); the build is LLM-only. During a build, the only thing the human knows that the agent doesn't is what the experience should feel like. Technical questions (how to fix a compilation error, which library to use, how to structure the code) are the agent's domain. Experience questions (should items without a due date appear at the top or bottom?) require the user's judgment. This boundary keeps the human in their zone of authority and the agent in its zone of authority.

**Why an async inbox in the viewer instead of queuing ideas in the CLI?**

The CLI (Claude Code) is the conversation surface — it's where evolve discussions happen. But ideas don't arrive during conversations; they arrive at random moments: browsing the web, using a competitor's product, thinking in the shower. The viewer is always accessible (a browser tab) and always connected to the project. Making it an input surface for async items means the user can capture ideas the moment they occur, without switching to the terminal, without interrupting a build, without losing context. The inbox is a queue, not a conversation — it doesn't try to replace the CLI for spec discussions, just captures raw input for later processing.

**Why a version registry instead of ad-hoc version management?**

Projects have multiple versions (project version, spec version, potentially API version) that appear in multiple files. Without a registry, version bumps require knowing which files to update and remembering to update all of them — a manual, error-prone process. The version registry centralizes this: declare version types and their propagation targets once, and every version change updates everything automatically. This is especially critical for the target user (non-coders) who shouldn't need to know that a version number appears in `package.json`, `plugin.json`, and a README badge. Auto-discovery at first execute reduces manual configuration, while declared targets ensure propagation is predictable. Relationship rules between version types (e.g., major spec change → external minor bump) make the version ecosystem self-consistent without the user managing cross-version dependencies.

**Why does the spec viewer auto-start silently instead of requiring `/fctry:view`?**

Observability should be always available, not opt-in. The viewer runs on a plugin hook that fires on every prompt, but the `ensure` logic makes it a no-op (<5ms) when no spec exists or the viewer is already running — so it never slows anything down. Auto-start uses `--no-open` to avoid surprise browser tabs; the user runs `/fctry:view` when they want to actually look at the viewer. The server persists across sessions because it serves all projects — killing it on one session end would disrupt monitoring of other projects. Self-healing on the next prompt means no orphaned-process management needed. The result: the viewer is always ready when the user wants it, never in the way when they don't.

**Why a single multi-project server instead of per-project servers?**

A user with many projects (the target persona) would otherwise accumulate separate server processes, each on a different port, each requiring its own browser tab. That's operational overhead that contradicts the "co-founder with perfect memory" vision. A single server with a project sidebar lets the user see all their projects at a glance and switch instantly — the same mental model as a multi-tab IDE or a project management dashboard. It also creates the natural foundation for a future dashboard view (overview of all projects) and for the viewer evolving into a control plane. The tradeoff is increased server complexity and a global lifecycle (persists across sessions, must self-heal), but the user experience is dramatically simpler.

---

## Appendix B: Glossary

| Term | Meaning |
|------|---------|
| **Experience language** | Descriptions of what users see, do, and feel — never databases, APIs, or code. Example: "The user sees a list of items sorted by urgency, with overdue items highlighted." |
| **NLSpec v2** | Natural Language Specification format, version 2. A structured template for describing software systems in experience language. See `references/template.md`. |
| **Scenario** | A user story that describes a complete journey through the system, written in experience language. Scenarios form the holdout set used to evaluate satisfaction. |
